<div id="review_2536968489" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2536968489" />
    <a title="Konstantin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/60906430-konstantin"><img alt="Konstantin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1537659835p2/60906430.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2536968489?book_show_action=true">Jan 02, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Konstantin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Konstantin" href="/user/show/60906430-konstantin">Konstantin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2536968489" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4079124962476130235">Sleazy, overhyped and ineffectively dramatic. I felt like the violence and the long 'flashback' served no real purpose. This didn't lean either into philosophical and metaphorical area (many of the characterizations are lazy) nor into the graphic horror one (I felt like I could've blinked and missed the violent part; it reads exactly like what it is - a middle-aged man's weird sexual fantasy).<br/><br/>I do appreciate what Murakami tried to show with this novel and how many themes he incorporated, as well</span>
  <span id="freeText4079124962476130235" style="display:none">Sleazy, overhyped and ineffectively dramatic. I felt like the violence and the long 'flashback' served no real purpose. This didn't lean either into philosophical and metaphorical area (many of the characterizations are lazy) nor into the graphic horror one (I felt like I could've blinked and missed the violent part; it reads exactly like what it is - a middle-aged man's weird sexual fantasy).<br /><br />I do appreciate what Murakami tried to show with this novel and how many themes he incorporated, as well as what kind of a bar it set for writers of the genre - Japan and elsewhere, but I think that I've just consumed too many of its pastiches (and just genuinely similar novels) that by this point I've seen it all before - and done better, as well.<br /><br />The blurbs promised me a "neon noir thought-piece" , yet I felt all of those aspects were lacking. Neither the descriptions nor the aesthetics felt vibrant; and setting up an atmosphere in my point of view requires more than "we walked down a dark, narrow alley". For a good character study with bits of sexual-oriented gore and violence, I wholeheartedly recommend <i>Peach</i> by Emma Glass.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4079124962476130235" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_44962912" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/44962912" />
    <a title="christa" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/40816-christa"><img alt="christa" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1349499447p2/40816.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/44962912?book_show_action=true">Jan 31, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="christa" class="user" itemprop="url" name="christa" href="/user/show/40816-christa">christa</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer44962912" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17942629785677817450">"In The Miso Soup" by Ryu Murakami is not the kind of book that you bring home to meet your parents. It is lurid. It is frightening. It is unpredictable. Murakami plucked ordinary words out of nowhere and arranged them into a simple, matter-of-fact horror. Like a smiling child with a box of crayons, humming the Dora the Explorer theme song, then showing you a crudely drawn crime scene with headless chickens and bloody axes and dead parents.<br/><br/>It is a delicious read.<br/><br/>Kenji has the slightly illegal jo</span>
  <span id="freeText17942629785677817450" style="display:none">"In The Miso Soup" by Ryu Murakami is not the kind of book that you bring home to meet your parents. It is lurid. It is frightening. It is unpredictable. Murakami plucked ordinary words out of nowhere and arranged them into a simple, matter-of-fact horror. Like a smiling child with a box of crayons, humming the Dora the Explorer theme song, then showing you a crudely drawn crime scene with headless chickens and bloody axes and dead parents.<br /><br />It is a delicious read.<br /><br />Kenji has the slightly illegal job of guiding tourists through Japan's underground night life. He knows where to find peep shows, lap dances, sex, and places where teenage girls are paid to just talk to men. He is hired by Frank, an American, for a three-night job ending on New Year's Eve. Kenji is immediately on alert around his client, who always seems to be lying, who's skin seems cold and plastic, who occasionally gets a dark and violent look in his eyes. The news is filled with the story of a Japanese school girl known for "selling it" who was found dismembered. Kenji's senses that Frank was involved.<br /><br />After their first night together, Kenji wakes to news that a homeless man was found burned to death. And when he leaves his house, Kenji finds a postage-sized stamp of something he believes is charred skin stuck to his door. Frank. <br /><br />This is such a great book. While I always carry a book in my purse, this is the first time in a long time that I pulled it out for quick reads while walking through the skyway and waiting in line at Subway. And when a Jehovah's Witness tried to hand me a pamphlet when I was face deep in the muck, I chuckled and wondered what he would think if he could see the words I was reading at that moment. <br /><br />There is a full-on mass murder scene midway through this book that is written so easily and so visually that I gagged. For instance "... Seeing the throat yawn open like the hood of a car." I love books that affect me physically. Like in "The Gargoyle" when my legs went numb reading about the main character's burning flesh. I guess my favorite genre would have to be "stuff that makes me want to barf."<br /><br />I first read a book by Ryu Murakami when I was taking an international fiction class in college. I remember little from "Almost Transparent Blue" beyond sex, drugs and cockroaches. Although I believe that the cockroaches were actually the cleanest part of that trifecta. I remember being stunned by the book. Liking it, but being shocked. I had never read anything like it and 10 years later it is still stuck in my head. I'm not sure why I never doubled back to read more by Ryu Murakami. That is about to change.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17942629785677817450" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1206250889?book_show_action=true">Feb 20, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/40477722-liv?shelf=2016-reading-challenge">2016-reading-challenge</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/40477722-liv?shelf=books-similar-to-haruki-murakami-s">books-similar-to-haruki-murakami-s</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1206250889" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer498640296062051819">Beautifully written, unsettling, disturbing, and unforgettable. Loneliness is a major theme throughout In the Miso Soup. Loneliness of Japanese and American culture is looked at through the eyes of Kenji and Frank - a pair who form an odd, and jarring bond that pulls you into the story. Kenji takes Frank (an American tourist) on a guided tour through Tokyo's nightlife and sex industry, only for Kenji to begin to suspect Frank of murdering a high school student. As per usual with Ryu Murakami, th</span>
  <span id="freeText498640296062051819" style="display:none">Beautifully written, unsettling, disturbing, and unforgettable. Loneliness is a major theme throughout In the Miso Soup. Loneliness of Japanese and American culture is looked at through the eyes of Kenji and Frank - a pair who form an odd, and jarring bond that pulls you into the story. Kenji takes Frank (an American tourist) on a guided tour through Tokyo's nightlife and sex industry, only for Kenji to begin to suspect Frank of murdering a high school student. As per usual with Ryu Murakami, there's always one frightfully disturbing scene that makes your skin crawl, and your stomach queazy. This is not a book for those who turn pale at detailed descriptions of violence or sexual violence. </span>
  <a data-text-id="498640296062051819" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9784770029577" href="/book/show/830976.In_the_Miso_Soup">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/185835-yulia?shelf=japandemonium">japandemonium</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3013055" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17632446994190797648">In the Miso Soup follows the 20-year-old tour guide Kenji in the nights leading up to New Yearâs as he shows Japanâs sex district to an American tourist who happens to be a pathological liar and may be responsible for the grisly murder and cutting-up-in-bits of a school-age prostitute (yes, your favorite kind). <br/><br/>Unlike other page turners, In the Miso Soup creates a story that never comes at the cost of character. Murakami has an impressive ability to convey individuals through a select number of </span>
  <span id="freeText17632446994190797648" style="display:none">In the Miso Soup follows the 20-year-old tour guide Kenji in the nights leading up to New Yearâs as he shows Japanâs sex district to an American tourist who happens to be a pathological liar and may be responsible for the grisly murder and cutting-up-in-bits of a school-age prostitute (yes, your favorite kind). <br /><br />Unlike other page turners, In the Miso Soup creates a story that never comes at the cost of character. Murakami has an impressive ability to convey individuals through a select number of details, none superfluous or ostentatiously idiosyncratic. Instead, Murakami masterfully presents the subtlety and contradiction even of side characters.<br /><br />Like most narrators written by authors older than the characters they give voice to, Kenji displays insight beyond his years and a keen awareness of and desire to understand those around him without either judging them or making them come across as âtypes.â While raising important questions about our cultural assumptions and values as well as our consumerism, Kenji is also never caught being pedantic or preachy. How do you do it, Murakami, um, Kenji? <br /><br />And somehow, amid all the tension, it manages to be funny. Even better, unlike Murakami's more recent book, Pierced, this one never made me squeamish or wince. In my opinion, this is by far the strongest of his works and deserves a place alongside the best of the other Murakami. </span>
  <a data-text-id="17632446994190797648" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3141354411" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7252848313217473267"><b>ETA:</b> [Dieses Buch haben wir auch im <i>Papierstau Podcast</i> besprochen (<a href='https://papierstaupodcast.de/podcast/folge-87' rel='nofollow noopener'>Folge 87: Murakami vs. Murakami</a>)] <b>/ETA</b><br/><br/>My first Murakami (either one), and I'd say this was a good introduction to the work and world of Mr. RyÅ« M. This is a fast, addictive read. It sucked me in right from the start and never let got - the pacing was just perfect for this book.<br/><br/>We follow Kenji, a specialized tourist guide - he shows interested, curious or plain horny foreigners the variety of Tokyo's nightlife scene. His latest cu</span>
  <span id="freeText7252848313217473267" style="display:none"><b>ETA:</b> [Dieses Buch haben wir auch im <i>Papierstau Podcast</i> besprochen (<a href="https://papierstaupodcast.de/podcast/folge-87" rel="nofollow noopener">Folge 87: Murakami vs. Murakami</a>)] <b>/ETA</b><br /><br />My first Murakami (either one), and I'd say this was a good introduction to the work and world of Mr. RyÅ« M. This is a fast, addictive read. It sucked me in right from the start and never let got - the pacing was just perfect for this book.<br /><br />We follow Kenji, a specialized tourist guide - he shows interested, curious or plain horny foreigners the variety of Tokyo's nightlife scene. His latest customer, an American named Frank, gives Kenji pretty much the creeps right from the start: Many of Frank's stories doen't really add up, he sometimes has this weird look on his face, and what's with the dead young girl found the night before - could that be Frank's doing? <br /><br />As random as Kenji's suspicions seem at first, he talks himself into them more and more and we, the reader, get sucked into Kenji's growing worries and fear. We are, in a way, like Kenji himself: We not only delve deeper into the shady nightlife district and its exotic characters, we also grow more and more weary of Frank and his antics. And yet, we can't stop either. We have to go on, bear more and read on. <br /><br />It is, however, also a very dark and violent read. It may contain one of the most brutal scenes I've ever read, and I'm not easily shocked. However, this got me, not just because of its very graphic description, for this isn't mindless splatter. Rather, it offers a quite unique glimpse into the soul of a tortured, psychotic character, and in the end, it wasn't the gore I was pondering, it was the mixture of cause and effect, of fruitless treatments and wrong doings and - the question whether any of this is believable at all.<br /><br />This book reads like a terrible nightmare you're caught in - you know you're dreaming, and even though you're in great fear, you force yourself to dream on to see what happens. Interesting, weird, brutal and yet very addictive.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7252848313217473267" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

I liked the beginning, but it built up to a climax that felt hollow and meaningless.   <span id="freeText8695617323917943888" style="display:none">If you cut out about 10 to 20% of this book, my review would probably be higher. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/17810.In_the_Miso_Soup" title="In the Miso Soup by RyÅ« Murakami" rel="nofollow noopener">In the Miso Soup</a> is the disturbing, paranoid tale of Kenji, whose job is to show tourists looking for the kind of entertainment Tokyo's dirty back streets and sex trade provide. He's been hired by Frank to show him where to have a good time. Kenji is passive, unambitious and kind of annoying. Frank is a monster.<br /><br />The book succeeds in creating a pervasive, inescapable sense of dread, as Kenji becomes more and more convinced his client is more than just another Western sex tourist. I enjoyed the creepy buildup and the mounting evidence.<br /><br />Where it fails - for me - is in the violence. There were scenes of cruelty and torture so extreme they made me sick to my stomach. Horror is one of my favorite genres. But there's a difference between horror and torture porn. Parts of this book were just that.</span>

  <span id="freeText15876362070723146158" style="display:none">I remember reading this book many years ago, back when I was living in France. Despite its content, it didn't stop me from moving to Tokyo.<br />"In the Miso Soup" is very graphic, violent, and at times downright scary. But the real strength of the books lies in the relationship between the two main protagonists: Kenji and Frank and the dialogues.<br />"In the Miso Soup" is a creepy, unforgettable novel and one of my favorites from Ryu Murakami. Whatever it is, <i>In The Miso Soup</i> is one of the most visceral and cerebral books you'll ever read. RyÅ« Murakami combines philosophical nihilism, existentialism, surrealism, disillusion and black comedy to craft an overwhelmingly disturbing and thoughtful novel.<br/></span>

<div id="review_2354090089" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2354090089" />
    <a title="Tim" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7202176-tim"><img alt="Tim" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1324164999p2/7202176.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2354090089?book_show_action=true">Apr 07, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tim" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tim" href="/user/show/7202176-tim">Tim</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7202176-tim?shelf=japanese">japanese</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7202176-tim?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7202176-tim?shelf=1990s">1990s</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2354090089" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4705551726382422526">Well, this is not what I expected it to be. I mean if you look at the plot description, it certainly gives you a pretty good clue of what to expect, and yet it absolutely fails to convey the type of novel you are getting into. Think philosophy novel, with a serial killer spin, which gives you both horrific violence and scenes of modernity examined in long conversation. In fact, if you examine the short 217 page count, I would say almost half of it is one really long conversation. It is a fascina</span>
  <span id="freeText4705551726382422526" style="display:none">Well, this is not what I expected it to be. I mean if you look at the plot description, it certainly gives you a pretty good clue of what to expect, and yet it absolutely fails to convey the type of novel you are getting into. Think philosophy novel, with a serial killer spin, which gives you both horrific violence and scenes of modernity examined in long conversation. In fact, if you examine the short 217 page count, I would say almost half of it is one really long conversation. It is a fascinating conversation, and one you are not meant to agree with, but fascinating in the approach and examination of its subject.<br /><br />The book examines the differences between America and Japan and how the two view each other. While written by a Japanese author, he makes some observations that feelâ¦ well, very American from his American characters. He truly does examine some of the aspects of Japanese culture that many Americans are baffled by and he points out that neither he nor his characters can find any way to explain these aspects. They fall into a large category that he sums up as âWe think it is coolâ and canât offer any deeper understand, which seems just as baffling to him as his American characters. Thereâs many thoughtful moments along the way in which he seems to be viewing his own country as an outsider and trying to explain âwhyâ it functions in such a way. <br /><br />This is a book that almost needs to be viewed in metaphoric terms. For example: <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[our narrator mentions early on about how aids is considered a âforeignerâ disease, and our American psycho Frank is preying on people in the seediest district of townâ¦ now what could this possibly be discussing?  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />One thing I have to compliment, Murakami is excellent with his charactersâ voices. It doesnât matter if it is a teenage girl, a serial killer or our lost narrator, everyone has their own unique outlook and voice. Though there isnât a large cast, many small characters enter the picture and they only feel repetitive when that reinforces the point. <br /><br />Iâve only read one other Ryu Murakami book and that was âPopular Hits of the Showa Era.â I found that one a more enjoyable book, but this was probably the âbetterâ one. I gave the other book a higher rating though as enjoyment counts for points from me. It was a silly ride while still making its point (and for those interested in such things, it was a more violent book, but not as graphic as this one). Both books speak about different aspects of Japan in a dark, almost illogical way, but this one seems more concerned with conveying its point. It is a more literary work, and one Iâm glad I experienced, but will likely never want to revisit. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="4705551726382422526" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1574588268" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1574588268" />
    <a title="Emm CÂ²" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/53296236-emm-c"><img alt="Emm CÂ²" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1558724665p2/53296236.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1574588268?book_show_action=true">May 02, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Emm CÂ²" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Emm CÂ²" href="/user/show/53296236-emm-c">Emm CÂ²</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/53296236-emm-c?shelf=mystery">mystery</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/53296236-emm-c?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/53296236-emm-c?shelf=horror">horror</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/53296236-emm-c?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/53296236-emm-c?shelf=thriller">thriller</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/53296236-emm-c?shelf=adult-and-new-adult">adult-and-new-adult</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1574588268" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16506325779669493069"> As much of a GIANT SPOILER as the blurb is, still it doesn't really prepare you for <i>In the Miso Soup</i>. Don't confuse this author with <i>Haruki</i> Murakami, or you may be in for a surprise. Their styles are very different, and Ryu Murakami's tends to be much more candid and violent.<br/><br/> Surreal and pitch-dark, this novella gets written off as being only about the gore, or even trashy, and it doesn't deserve that. Yes, the graphic scenes are intense (particularly the nauseating <i>omiai</i> pub scene near the end</span>
  <span id="freeText16506325779669493069" style="display:none"> As much of a GIANT SPOILER as the blurb is, still it doesn't really prepare you for <i>In the Miso Soup</i>. Don't confuse this author with <i>Haruki</i> Murakami, or you may be in for a surprise. Their styles are very different, and Ryu Murakami's tends to be much more candid and violent.<br /><br /> Surreal and pitch-dark, this novella gets written off as being only about the gore, or even trashy, and it doesn't deserve that. Yes, the graphic scenes are intense (particularly the nauseating <i>omiai</i> pub scene near the end), but they're only a portion of the story.<br /> I think <i>Miso Soup</i> is a harsh criticism of society, Japan's and America's, their relationship with each other, and how they are both different and alike in a sort of social paradox. Especially in the ways that people are becoming more and more alienated from each other in both countries, which rings even more true now than in 1997 when this came out. All of Ryu Murakami's horror stories are a social critique of some kind. Of course, if you're in it <i>for</i> the creepy parts, there is no lack of them, believe me.<br /> It's pretty powerfully written for a horror. Kenji's growing paranoia and fear of this bizarre stranger, Frank (if that's even his real name) is all too realistic. Even more so when Frank starts to think of Kenji as a friend. A "friend" whom he can subtly threaten, lie to, and <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[kill in front of without worry <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>.<br /><br />Characterization - 5/5 <br /> They might be disturbing, but they are believable. Kenji is just an ordinary guy trying to make a living, albeit in a somewhat shady business, but hardly deserving of getting harrassed by a psychopath. Kenji isn't without a dark side of his own, which he is ashamed of whenever it comes to light.<br /> "Frank" is a terrifying character. He's so unpredictable and soullessly casual about everything, and he seems alien in mannerism. A really well-done antagonist, and character study on the nature of evil and empathy.<br /><br />Writing and Plotting - 5/5<br /> R. Murakami never writes poorly, that I have read. Some parts are not really thrilling, per se, but not uninteresting either. I feel like if this had been a longer book, it might've dragged, but it's just a bit past 200 pages.<br /><br />Originality - 4.5/5<br />General Score - 5/5<br /><br />Content Note - I would definitely <i>not</i> recommend if you are opposed or sensitive to graphic violence or graphic content in general. But if you don't mind that and like an unsettling mystery, give it a try.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16506325779669493069" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_272313446" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/272313446" />
    <a title="Leo Robertson" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7619789-leo-robertson"><img alt="Leo Robertson" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1571602096p2/7619789.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/272313446?book_show_action=true">Feb 04, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Leo Robertson" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Leo Robertson" href="/user/show/7619789-leo-robertson">Leo Robertson</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7619789-leo-robertson?shelf=reviewtastic">reviewtastic</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer272313446" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6295153503305794526">I write novels in my spare time- well, I've written one that's finished, and a pretty decent draft of a second one- you won't get any annoying updates from me though, there's plenty of that abound, and I'll be really careful to find the right audience and not bother anyone else...[1] Okay, it's been said somewhere, good [2].<br/><br/>What's my point though? Well... you can totally write about anything. I know you know this, but ANYTHING. Short stories about an alcoholic teacup. Microscopic space opera. <br/><br/>(T</span>
  <span id="freeText6295153503305794526" style="display:none">I write novels in my spare time- well, I've written one that's finished, and a pretty decent draft of a second one- you won't get any annoying updates from me though, there's plenty of that abound, and I'll be really careful to find the right audience and not bother anyone else...[1] Okay, it's been said somewhere, good [2].<br /><br />What's my point though? Well... you can totally write about anything. I know you know this, but ANYTHING. Short stories about an alcoholic teacup. Microscopic space opera. <br /><br />(These totally genius ideas are yours for free btw, and if you like them, stay tuned for what I actually chose to write about!)<br /><br />What's my point though? Haha! So you can write about anything, and that's great but also hugely overwhelming somehow, and then you come across a book which is so well put together and with such a clear vision it makes you say "Ah! I know what I want. I want to have written that." That's what I felt when I read Miso Soup.<br /><br />Sure, the cutaways and the drawn-out grander social messages are distracting and unnecessary, but... if only for the mood. If only because... you enter that world, and it's disgusting, dark, pessimistic, but you're not here, you're <i>there</i>, in the paper! And how rare is that? And what a test of good writing that is? What more needs to be said? And more questions?<br /><br />And <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/8881.Ry__Murakami" title="RyÅ« Murakami" rel="nofollow noopener">Murakami</a> pirouettes dangerously near the <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/28676.American_Psycho" title="American Psycho by Bret Easton Ellis" rel="nofollow noopener">American Psycho</a>[3] line of what's cool to put in a book, and it's really effective.<br /><br />Plus it's short. So, whatever, read it.<br /><br />[1] Can you tell I'm young and idealistic? How long do you give it, a month? :D<br />[2] Please don't let my reviews be any indication of my writing ability! Or if you like them, do :D<br />[3] Dude, get help.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6295153503305794526" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('QYSDm12qwBNrXxKpnS9HC/P5yGv7Ya+bpiZ+qT5RLqYneWgm4HGU+/OT+nob/EkmPuCUxnz+7im1685EMUZghA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('O5YQTAPZBi1BIwLhnXWzzLEdVAqvzvePfis62C3jABlda/vxvgJSxdnv6jIbpr3hfAQIpyhRtj1t5oo1IvROOw==')})
  }
//]]>

<span id="freeTextContainer14009831095928773716">RyÅ« <b>Murakami</b> (<a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/11015261._' rel='nofollow noopener'>æä¸ é¾</a>) is a Japanese novelist and filmmaker. He is not related to Haruki Murakami or Takashi Murakami.<br/><br/>Murakami's first work, the short novel Almost Transparent Blue, written while he was still a student, deals with promiscuity and drug use among disaffected Japanese youth. Critically acclaimed as a new style of literature, it won the newcomer's literature prize in 1976 despite some ob</span>
  <span id="freeText14009831095928773716" style="display:none">RyÅ« <b>Murakami</b> (<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/11015261._" rel="nofollow noopener">æä¸ é¾</a>) is a Japanese novelist and filmmaker. He is not related to Haruki Murakami or Takashi Murakami.<br /><br />Murakami's first work, the short novel Almost Transparent Blue, written while he was still a student, deals with promiscuity and drug use among disaffected Japanese youth. Critically acclaimed as a new style of literature, it won the newcomer's literature prize in 1976 despite some observers decrying it as decadent. Later the same year, Blue won the Akutagawa Prize, going on to become a best seller. In 1980, Murakami published the much longer novel Coin Locker Babies, again to critical acclaim.<br /><br />Takashi Miike's feature film Audition (1999) was based on one of his novels. Murakami reportedly liked it so much he gave Miike his blessing to adapt Coin Locker Babies. The screen play was worked on by director Jordan Galland. However, Miike could not raise funding for the project. An adaptation directed by Michele Civetta is currently in production.<br /><br />Murakami has played drums for a rock group called Coelacanth and hosted a TV talk show.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14009831095928773716" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>
